Morphological and functional characterization of host proteins during infections by actin - hijacking bacterial pathogens by Arandeep ( Aaron ) Singh Dhanda B . Sc . ( Hons ) , Simon Fraser University , 2013 Thesis Submitted in Partial Fulfillment of the Requirements for the Degree of Doctor of Philosophy in the Department of Biological Sciences Faculty of Science © Arandeep Singh Dhanda 2020 SIMON FRASER UNIVERSITY Spring 2020 Copyright in this work rests with the author . Please ensure that any reproduction or re - use is done in accordance with the relevant national copyright legislation . ii Approval Name : Arandeep ( Aaron ) Singh Dhanda Degree : Doctor of Philosophy ( Biological Sciences ) Title : Morphological and functional characterization of host proteins during infections by actin - hijacking bacterial pathogens Examining Committee : Chair : Nick Dulvy Professor Julian Guttman Senior Supervisor Professor Gordon Rintoul Supervisor Associate Professor Margo Moore Supervisor Professor Emerita Michael Silverman Internal Examiner Professor Alfredo Menendez External Examiner Associate Professor Department of Microbiology and Infectious Diseases University of Sherbrooke Date Defended / Approved : April 14 , 2020 iii Abstract Cells , much like mammals , possess an internal skeleton . This cellular skeleton ( called the cytoskeleton ) provides structure to cells , enables their movement within the environment and promotes the internalization of extracellular cargo ( endocytosis ) . Many pathogens have devised strategies to hijack the cytoskeleton and other crucial sub - cellular processes for their disease processes . The bacterium Listeria monocytogenes ( L . monocytogenes ) utilizes the clathrin endocytic machinery to invade cells , and later , the actin polymerization machinery to generate actin - rich comet / rocket tails to move within and amongst host cells . Salmonella enterica serovar Typhimurium ( S . Typhimurium ) and Shigella flexneri ( S . flexneri ) generate actin - rich membrane ruffles at the cell surface to enter cells . Once inside , S . Typhimurium occupies a long - lived vacuole , whereas S . flexneri generates comet / rocket tails . Enteropathogenic Escherichia coli ( EPEC ) on the other hand remain extracellular and co - opt clathrin and actin to form motile pedestals directly beneath the site of bacterial adherence . In this thesis , I explored the involvement of several host actin - and / or endocytic - associated proteins during bacterial infections and simultaneously used these infections to gain insight into novel roles of the proteins studied . In chapter 2 , I discovered that L . monocytogenes co - opts the actin - associated protein palladin during its entry and intracellular motility . Importantly , I revealed that palladin can functionally replace the Arp2 / 3 complex during bacterial actin - based motility . In chapter 3 , I uncovered that the internalization strategy used by L . monocytogenes to transfer between host cells exploits caveolin - mediated endocytosis . In chapter 4 , I investigated the host enzyme cyclophilin A ( CypA ) and found that it is crucial for maintaining the structural integrity of L . monocytogenes membrane protrusions generated during bacterial dissemination events . In chapter 5 , I determined that CypA restricts S . Typhimurium invasion but is dispensable for EPEC pedestal formation . Finally , in chapters 6 and 7 , I examined the receptor of CypA , CD147 , and found that this membrane protein , like CypA , is crucial for the proper formation and function of L . monocytogenes membrane protrusions . In conclusion , my research has 2 major implications : 1 ) I have uncovered new insight into the mechanisms behind how actin - hijacking pathogens cause disease and 2 ) I have demonstrated novel cellular functions for host actin - associated proteins . iv Keywords : Actin ; caveolin ; Listeria monocytogenes ; EPEC ; Shigella flexneri ; Salmonella enterica serovar Typhimurium v Dedication I dedicate this work to my parents , sisters and grandparents who have given me such unwavering support as I complete this chapter of my life . Additionally , I truly could not have accomplished this study without the fierce mentorship , encouragement and support from my supervisors , colleagues and students . On a final note , I leave my graduate studies , not alone , but together with my fiancée , Kiran Heer . You have been an amazing beacon of encouragement during the conclusion of my degree and I simply cannot wait for what life has in store for the both of us . vi Acknowledgements Although it might go without saying , I shall express it anyways . Introduction 1 . 1 . A brief history of actin Actin is one of the most abundant proteins found in eukaryotic cells , often accounting for ~ 10 % of total protein in metazoans 1 . Thus , it is not surprising that many essential cell biological processes such as cellular motility ( and adhesion ) 2 – 5 , intracellular transport 6 – 9 , organization of cell polarity 10 , 11 , and cytokinesis 5 , 10 – 12 , all rely on this ubiquitous family of proteins . In fact , the laundry list of actin - mediated functions is continuously expanding as more recent studies have uncovered crucial roles for actin during DNA repair 13 , 14 and even glycolysis 15 . The roots of actin can be traced back to mid twentieth century biochemical studies conducted by Albert Szent - Györgyi and colleagues who were the first to label a novel muscle tissue ( and myosin - interacting ) protein as ‘actin’ 16 . These early biochemical studies on muscle actin ( and myosin ) culminated in the first in vitro reconstitution of the muscle contractile machinery 16 , 17 . Perhaps just as important , this early work on muscle actin uncovered that in the absence or presence of salts , actin can exist in 2 forms , as single molecules ( or monomers ) termed globular actin ( G - actin ) or as long fibers ( F - actin ) respectively ( Figure 1 . 1 ) 16 , 18 . We now know that it is this intrinsic ability of actin to exist in these 2 forms that enables it to function during various cellular processes . These seminal studies paved the way for future actin research . It wasn’t until 1966 when Hatano and Oosawa identified actin in non - muscle cells 19 . Biology’s first glimpse of actin within the cell relied on early electron microscopy imaging , and in particular , thin sectioning techniques 20 – 22 . These first images provided a two - dimensional glimpse of F - actin arranged into what appeared like large bundles or fibers 20 , 22 . These initial cellular observations of actin were in fact the actin cytoskeleton , the complex network of actin filaments and other actin - associated proteins that are organized within the cell . To reveal in more structural insight , the three - dimensional structure of the actin cytoskeleton was explored . Here , researchers utilized metal replica electron microscopy 23 – 25 . These electron microscopic images provided a high - resolution view of various actin cytoskeletal components such as the migrating edge of cells ( the lamellipodium ) and thin protruding processes called filopodia 23 – 25 . With the advent of 2 immunofluorescence microscopy and development of various fluorescent probes in conjunction with actin - targeting antibodies and the purification and use of mushroom phallotoxins such as phalloidin 26 , researchers were given another avenue to examine cellular actin in more detail . Current developments such as the use of DNA plasmids encoding small fluorescently - tagged actin - binding peptides , such as LifeAct , allow researchers to examine in real - time the subcellular dynamics of F - actin within cells and tissues 27 . The development of confocal ( and now super - resolution ) microscopy platforms has enhanced our view of the actin cytoskeleton to the molecular level 28 , 29 . Genetically , humans ( and other vertebrates ) express three main isoforms of actin , α - actin , β - actin and γ - actin . Humans possess 3 α - actin genes , which are expressed in muscle ( skeletal , smooth and cardiac ) , 1 β - actin gene expressed in non - muscle cells and 2 γ - actin genes ( 1 in smooth muscle and 1 in non - muscle cells ) 5 , 30 . The actin gene is highly conserved among metazoans as it varies by only a few amino acids between animals , fungi and algae 5 , 31 . In fact , human γ - actin and the single yeast ( Schizosaccharomyces pombe ) actin share ~ 91 . 2 % identity 31 . This high level of conservation likely arose because of the crucial role of actin in cells . Specifically , Pollard and colleagues propose that , since metazoan cells rely on actin to polymerize into filaments as well as maintain interactions with an abundant array of other cellular proteins , physical constraints such as ensuring the ability of actin to properly polymerize into stable and functional actin filaments as well as interact with its various binding partners , likely governs this genetic conservation 5 . Genetic duplication of the actin gene has given rise to a group of actin - related proteins commonly referred to as “Arps” 32 . At least 11 Arps ( Arp1 - Arp11 ) have been identified that possess up to ~ 50 % sequence identity with actin . Of particular interest are the Arp2 and Arp3 proteins as they belong to a 7 - subunit - containing complex known as the Arp2 / 3 complex , which is involved in generating branched arrays of F - actin ( explained in detail below ) . In addition to the Arps , several other eukaryotic proteins ( such as hexokinase and Hsc70 ) share structural 3D similarities with monomeric actin 33 , 34 . These proteins all possess a highly similar ATP - binding ATPase domain ( composed of 5 separate motifs ) 34 . Interestingly , many prokaryotes also express actin - like ( and ATPase domain - containing ) proteins 35 such as MreB 36 , ParM 37 , 38 and FtsA 39 . Spirals of polymerized MreB function to maintain the shape of bacterial cells 36 whereas ParM segregates plasmid DNA 37 , 38 and FtsA regulates cell division 39 . 3 Below , I will provide additional insight into G - and F - actin , how F - actin is generated , the role of actin - associated proteins during F - actin formation as well as a brief overview of several key actin cytoskeletal elements ( such as the lamellipodium ) that are generated in eukaryotic cells . 1 . 2 . Monomeric actin Eukaryotic actin molecules consist of a 375 amino acid polypeptide and have a molecular weight of ~ 42 kDa . The co - crystalization of G - actin - DNase I complexes ( instead of pure actin , as the propensity of pure actin to polymerize precluded crystal formation ) by Kabsch and colleagues provided the first high resolution structure of actin 40 . From this study , actin was described as having 2 major domains , with each domain further divided into 2 subdomains 40 . Actin binds ATP ( or ADP ) and this interaction occurs in the cleft found between the 2 major domains of actin 41 . Tight binding ( in the nanomolar K d range ) between ATP ( or ADP ) and actin requires the presence of Ca 2 + or Mg 2 + ions 5 . Arguably the most important feature of actin is its ability to form long thin polymers . In the presence of divalent ( Mg 2 + ) or monovalent ( K + or Na + ) ions , G - actin will self - polymerize into filaments ( F - actin ) 16 , 18 , 41 . Conversely , in the absence of salts , F - actin will depolymerize into actin monomers . Although self - polymerization can occur spontaneously , polymerization begins with a lag period , leading up to the formation of short stable oligomers that precede fast assembly of longer filaments ( Figure 1 . 1 ) 42 . As we will see later , a decrease in this lag period as well as increased assembly of F - actin can occur in the presence of certain actin - associated proteins . Importantly , the concentration of free actin monomers is also crucial to filament elongation as a high concentration of G - actin favours filament assembly 4 , 4 , 41 , 42 . More recent advances in actin studies have examined post - translational modifications ( PTMs ) of G - actin 43 as well as the transport and functional role of actin in the nuclear environment 13 , 44 – 46 . Actin molecules can undergo several PTMs such as acetylation , arginylation , O - GlcNAcylation , phosphorylation and ubiquitination 43 . Actin in the nucleus predominantly exists as monomeric actin 14 ; however , nuclear actin filaments have been observed on occasion 14 . The transport of actin into the nucleus is mediated by the actin - binding protein cofilin and importin 9 45 . Currently , the major function of monomeric actin in the nucleus revolves around chromatin - remodelling 44 . Astonishingly , despite the 4 general absence of F - actin within the nucleus , new insight into DNA repair mechanisms link F - actin , and not G - actin , to these processes 13 . 1 . 3 . Filamentous actin – actin polymerization dynamics The transformation of G - actin into F - actin , and vice versa , governs most cellular processes that rely on actin . One crucial function of F - actin is its ability to transduce force to generate motility of the cell . Cellular migration 2 – 4 , endocytosis 6 and even DNA strand repair 13 all rely on the ability of F - actin to generate propulsive forces and / or relocalize particles . Generally , the way this occurs is via the polymerization of actin filaments into networked arrays ( Figure 1 . 2 ) 4 , 6 , 42 . In the cell , ATP - bound actin molecules join together ( head - to - tail ) eventually forming 3 - 4 subunit length oligomers ( or seeds ) . At this stage elongation of the actin seed into long filaments occurs . The head - to - tail polymerization of actin gives the filament structural polarity 4 , 5 , 41 , 42 . This polarity can be observed clearly in myosin decoration experiments of F - actin 5 , 47 – 49 . One end is termed the barbed ( + , plus ) end and the other the pointed ( - , minus ) end . Crucially , actin subunits are added much more rapidly at the barbed end then they are at the minus end 4 , 5 , 42 . This is due to the differing critical concentration ( C c ) values ( when the concentration of the G - actin pool is in equilibrium with F - actin ) at each polar end . The C c value at the barbed end is ~ 0 . 1 µM and ~ 0 . 8 µM at the minus end 1 . Essentially , when critical concentrations are high , G - actin is added to both ends . At intermediate values , G - actin is only added to the barbed end . When C c values are below 0 . 1 µM , no filament elongation occurs . It is at these more intermediate C c values where G - actin is continuously being turned over at the filament . The simultaneous addition and removal of actin monomers , termed treadmilling , underlies the mechanism behind actin force propulsion found at the leading edge ( lamellipodium ) of migrating cells 4 , 50 . However , treadmilling of pure actin in vitro occurs very slowly 4 . Thus , the cell must rely on other actin - associated factors to accelerate actin filament treadmilling ( Figure 1 . 2 ) . One such factor is the actin - binding protein profilin , which brings ATP - G - actin to the barbed end for insertion into the growing filament 5 , 41 . ATP hydrolysis ultimately results in ADP - actin at the pointed end of filaments . Other actin - associated proteins ( see below ) , remove these ADP - actin molecules from the pointed end . To recirculate these ADP - actin molecules , profilin catalyses the exchange of ADP for ATP and thus transports newly ATP - bound G - actin molecules to the growing barbed end 4 . 5 As previously mentioned , the cell utilizes actin to carry out many vital biological processes such as cell migration 2 – 4 and endocytosis 6 . In order for actin to promote these crucial processes , F - actin must be organized into one of two specific types of networked arrays , 1 ) tightly packed bundles of unbranched actin filaments ( termed parallel F - actin ) and 2 ) highly dynamic branched meshwork’s of F - actin ( termed branched F - actin ) . To generate these complex structures , cells rely on a group of robust actin - associated proteins commonly referred to actin nucleators . The seven subunit - containing Arp2 / 3 complex is well known to generate branched F - actin arrays 23 , 51 , 52 whereas members of the formin family of actin nucleators direct the formation of unbranched actin filaments 53 – 56 . Together , with an assortment of actin - associated proteins , these actin nucleators establish a functional actin cytoskeleton within the cell . 1 . 3 . 1 . Branched actin – Arp2 / 3 - mediated actin polymerization Identification and characterization of the Arp2 / 3 complex largely took off in the early 1990’s with the pioneering research of Thomas Pollard and colleagues who purified a 7 - polypeptide complex , now recognized as the Arp2 / 3 complex , from the amoeba Acanthamoeba castellan ( Acanthamoeba ) 57 . The Arp2 / 3 complex consists of Arp3 , Arp2 , p41 / ARPC1 , p34 / ARPC2 , p21 / ARPC3 , p20 / ARPC4 and p16 / ARPC5 ( note : the “p # # ” designation indicates the apparent molecular weight of the initial subunits purified as determined by immunoblotting ) . Biochemical studies demonstrated that the Arp2 / 3 complex contains one copy each of the seven subunits 58 . Of particular interest was the discovery that the Arp2 and Arp3 subunits share sequence and structural identity to actin 57 . Importantly , research out of the Pollard lab confirmed actin - binding by the Arp2 / 3 complex as the complex was found attached to the sides of preformed actin filaments by electron microscopy 58 . Some of the earliest studies at the ultrastructural level had uncovered that the motile edge of eukaryotic cells ( lamellipodium ) consisted of a dense network of highly branched F - actin 24 , 59 – 61 . These early micrographs depicted a highly interwoven network of actin filaments at the motile leading edge of cells with what appeared to be electron dense clusters attached to the sides of filaments as well as at the branching point of two filaments . In 1998 , Pollard and colleagues had finally made the breakthrough in identifying the Arp2 / 3 complex as the factor that could bind to the sides of actin filaments at their pointed ends , protecting them from disassembly and , amazingly , nucleate new actin 6 filaments branches that would grow outwards from their barbed ends 51 . Remarkably , these new daughter filaments grew away from their site of attachment on the mother filaments at a consistent 70° angle 51 . Approximately a year later , the Arp2 / 3 complex was immunolocalized at the ultrastructural level at the exact branching site of the characteristic y - shaped and 70° angle actin filaments within the lamellipodium 23 . Functionally however , the Arp2 / 3 complex alone is intrinsically inactive and thus requires the presence of another class of proteins , called the nucleation promoting factors ( NPFs ) , to become active 62 , 63 . Classically , the Neural Wiskott - Aldrich Syndrome protein ( N - WASp ) is depicted as the activator of Arp2 / 3 62 – 66 . However , the first activator of Arp2 / 3 characterized was the Listeria monocytogenes ( L . monocytogenes ) surface protein , ActA , which structurally mimics eukaryotic N - WASP 52 , 67 . As we will see in subsequent sections , activation of the Arp2 / 3 complex by ActA ( and other bacterial proteins ) allows intracellular ( and extracellular ) bacteria to generate branched actin - containing motile structures . Additional eukaryotic Arp2 / 3 NPFs have been discovered such as cortactin 63 , 68 – 71 , Abp1 72 – 74 , Scar / WAVE 63 , 75 – 83 , WASH 63 , 84 – 89 , WHAMM 63 , 90 and JMY 63 , 91 . While Arp2 / 3 and its associated NPFs are classically considered as cytosolic , evidence of nuclear - localized F - actin , Arp2 / 3 , WASP and JMY reveal an increased complexity in how Arp2 / 3 - mediated actin polymerization is regulated 13 , 46 , 92 . Some of the major actin - based elements generated in cells using the Arp2 / 3 complex ( see below ) , include motile lamellipodia 23 , 61 , 93 , 94 , endocytic invaginations 6 , 7 , 95 – 99 , adhesive ( and matrix degrading ) podosomes / invadopodia 100 – 104 as well as several types of motile bacterially - derived structures 52 , 105 – 110 . 1 . 3 . 2 . Parallel actin – Formin - mediated actin polymerization Only a few higher order actin structures , such as subset of stress fibers ( see below ) 111 , 112 , rely solely on parallel actin arrays . Rather , the majority of cellular ( and bacterially - derived ) actin structures depend on cooperation between both branched and parallel actin polymerization machineries for their proper formation 23 , 93 , 94 , 113 – 115 . An Arp2 / 3 - independent mechanism promotes the generation of unbranched actin filaments , which are later incorporated into parallel arrays . To accomplish this , the cell relies on a group of nucleators called the formin family of proteins 53 – 56 , 116 . 7 Formins are large multi - domain containing proteins 53 , 55 , 56 . The characteristic feature of formins is their formin homology 2 ( FH2 ) domain 56 , 117 – 119 . Importantly , formins dimerize , and it is this feature that is a prerequisite for the proper actin filament elongating activity of formins 56 , 120 – 125 . The dimerization of formins brings together the FH2 domains of each monomer into a ring - shaped structure 56 , 126 – 128 . Actin filament elongation by formins begins with the generation of short unstable actin seeds 54 . Electron microscopy studies revealed FH2 - mediated formin binding at the barbed ends of these short actin seeds 54 . At the barbed ends of actin filaments , these FH2 rings incorporate actin monomers processively , thus elongating the filament 54 . The formin homology 1 ( FH1 ) domain , another domain within formin , accelerates filament elongation by delivering G - actin - profilin complexes to FH2 bound at the filament barbed end 117 , 127 , 129 – 132 . The continued step - wise incorporation of actin molecules by formins generates long unbranched actin filaments . While evidence suggests formins can also directly bundle these filaments into larger parallel arrays 133 , 134 , most models depict a subset of actin - associated proteins ( termed actin crosslinkers ; see below ) which bundle actin . 1 . 4 . Actin - rich cellular structures Note : Many of the proteins described in this section will be reviewed in more detail in section 1 . 5 of this introduction . As noted above , the cell polymerizes actin into branched and parallel filament arrays which then organize into several complex well - defined structures . Two major functions of these dynamic structures are promoting cellular motility ( lamellipodia and filopodia ) and adhesion / mechanotransduction ( stress fibers ) ( Figures 1 . 3 - 1 . 4 ) . Two additional actin - rich structures described in this thesis ( and in more detail in the sections below ) are those generated during the internalization of extracellular cargo ( see section 1 . 6 . 3 ) and those induced by microbial pathogens during infections ( see sections 1 . 8 - 1 . 9 ) . It should be noted that the majority of the work presented in the following chapters of this thesis focuses heavily on these pathogen - derived actin - based structures ; however , insight into the aforementioned actin - rich cellular structures is crucial as these microbes frequently exploit the proteins that generate them . 8 1 . 4 . 1 . Lamellipodia In 1970 , Michael Abercrombie’s phase - contrast imaging of embryonic fibroblasts revealed a broad and flat , highly motile ruffle , at the edge of actively migrating cells 135 . In that same study , Abercrombie and colleagues coined the name “lamellipodium” for this protrusive structure . One year later , electron microscopic images revealed actin filaments with lamellipodia 20 . These pioneering studies paved the way for our current knowledge on how eukaryotic cells move in culture and in vivo . Actin polymerization at the extreme edge of the lamellipodium ( directly behind the plasma membrane ) , provides the propulsive forces required to move cells forward 51 , 63 , 136 – 141 . The major nucleator of actin at lamellipodia is the Arp2 / 3 complex 23 , 51 , 94 , 142 . Together with several other actin - associated proteins ( described in detail below ) such as the NPFs [ N - WASp / WAVE ] , VASP and cofilin , the Arp2 / 3 - based actin polymerization machinery generates a highly dynamic and rapidly turning over , meshwork of filamentous actin ( Figure 1 . 3 ) 4 , 42 . Several lines of evidence point towards a crucial role for the Arp2 / 3 complex during cell motility . Firstly , electron microscopic examination of the leading edge of cells reveals the 70° branches of actin filaments that are characteristic of the Arp2 / 3 complex 51 . Most importantly , in cells depleted of Arp2 / 3 , lamellipodia are absent and cellular motility is significantly perturbed 94 , 142 . Activation of the Arp2 / 3 complex begins at the plasma membrane . Following interactions between extracellular stimuli such as cytokines and growth factors and their corresponding receptors , several Rho family GTPases such as Cdc42 , Rac1 and RhoA are recruited and activated to the plasma membrane 139 . Actin polymerization by Arp2 / 3 begins following recruitment and activation of NPFs such as N - WASp and WAVE by these Rho GTPases 63 . 1 . 4 . 2 . Filopodia Filopodia are thin cellular projections involved in cell motility and exploratory processes ( Figure 1 . 3 ) 25 , 42 , 143 – 145 . In contrast to lamellipodia , actin filaments within filopodia are organized into tightly packed parallel bundles 25 , 143 , 144 , 146 . The major organizer of these actin filament bundles is fascin , as in cells depleted of fascin , fewer filopodia are generated and any remaining ones appear morphologically abnormal and collapse near 9 the cell surface 146 , 147 . Elongation of filopodia is mainly driven by the formin family of actin nucleators such as mDia2 148 – 151 . As in lamellipodia , actin nucleation is dependent on Rho GTPases , such as Cdc42 and RhoF / Rif 148 , 152 – 154 . Other actin - associated proteins such as VASP likely aid in the elongation of filaments as in cells inhibited of VASP , filopodia are fewer in number and shorter in length 155 – 159 . 1 . 4 . 3 . Stress Fibers Stress fibers are a prominent feature of many types of cultured animal cells ( Figure 1 . 4 ) 111 , 160 . Structurally , stress fibers consist of multiple actin filaments ( ~ 10 - 30 ) that are directly crosslinked together by α - actinin 160 . The relatively long actin filaments within stress fibers are generated via RhoA - mediated activation of the formin mDia1 131 , 160 – 165 . Functionally , stress fibers generate contractile forces within the cell 111 , 160 , 166 – 168 . The force for contraction is provided by the ATPase myosin II 160 . Thus , stress fibers play a key role during cellular processes that rely on tension and contraction , namely adhesion and mechanotransduction 160 , 169 – 173 . The exact role of stress fibers during cellular migration is less clear ; however , a subtype of stress fibers , termed transverse arc , are present within lamella , a broader array of linear actin filaments that is found behind lamellipodia 160 , 162 , 166 , 174 , 175 . Cellular adhesion to rigid surfaces , such as glass coverslips , provide the stress ( or tension ) necessary for stress fiber formation . Cells in vivo generally do not associate with such rigid substrates and thus the majority of cells in living animals do not possess stress fibers . However ; there exists one cell type which does in fact experience sufficient stresses that warrant the need for stress fiber formation . These are the endothelial cells which line blood vessels 176 . These stress fibers likely protect the endothelial lining from shear stresses experienced due to blood flow 177 . 1 . 5 . Important actin - associated proteins Actin alone ( and in the presence of salts ) self - polymerizes into filaments 18 and reconstitution assays that combine purified actin together with 5 other actin - associated proteins ( ADF / cofilin , profilin , capping protein , Arp2 / 3 and an NPF [ e . g . N - WASp ] ) generate branched actin arrays in vitro 107 . These in vitro arrays of actin exhibit force propulsion of particles 107 . Importantly , the actin filament arrays ( both branched and parallel ) generated inside the cell drive whole cell movement through lamellipodia and 10 filopodia 142 . While we have seen the major nucleators that drive the formation of these branched ( via Arp2 / 3 ) or parallel ( via formins ) F - actin array - containing cellular structures , the cell also depends on a more expansive arsenal of actin - associated proteins to regulate the dynamics of these cytoskeletal elements 4 , 5 , 178 , 179 . These actin - associated proteins belong to over 60 different classes 4 and perform a variety of functions within the actin - rich cellular structures . Below , I will review only a subset of classical actin - associated proteins as well as several proteins which pertain specifically to my research that is covered in later chapters . 1 . 5 . 1 . Filamentous actin stabilizers – crosslinkers and bundlers Although metazoan cells have evolved robust machineries to generate actin filaments , continued elongation will ultimately collapse them 4 , 5 , 180 , 181 . To overcome these physical constraints , cells possess a variety of actin - binding proteins that crosslink filaments along their entire length , thus increasing their overall rigidity 4 , 5 , 178 , 179 . Generally , these actin - binding proteins connect 2 separate filaments together . To accomplish this , actin crosslinkers ( and bundlers ) usually dimerize such as α - actinin 182 – 185 and palladin 186 – 188 as each monomer possess a single actin - binding domain . Alternatively , some actin crosslinkers , such as fascin 189 , 190 and fimbrin 191 , 192 possess 2 internal actin - binding domains . Higher - order actin structures such as lamellipodia , filopodia and stress fibers are further stabilized as these actin - binding proteins are studded along the majority of filaments within the networked array 4 , 5 , 178 , 179 . α - actinin In order to crosslink actin filaments , α - actinin molecules combine as anti - parallel homodimers 182 – 185 . Consequently , each end of the α - actinin dimer possesses actin - binding activity . Because of the relatively long spacer region which separates the two actin - binding domains , actin filaments bound by α - actinin are bundled less tightly ( with more open space between filaments ) than those crosslinked by proteins such as fimbrin or fascin ( see below ) . In the cell , α - actinin classically localizes to stress fibers as well as focal adhesions 193 – 197 . At stress fibers , the loosely packed filaments leave space for myosin integration which is necessary for efficient contractility of the cell 160 , 162 , 198 . The importance of α - actinin is highlighted by several observations of reduced stress fiber formation in cells absent ( or inhibited ) of α - actinin 199 . However , the role of α - actinin is not 11 strictly limited to stress fibers , as in cells depleted of α - actinin , widespread cytoskeletal alterations are also observed at lamellipodia and focal adhesions 200 . Palladin Palladin is an actin - binding protein that exists as 9 isoforms which are differentially expressed in a variety of tissues ( Figure 1 . 5 ) 201 – 203 . The 90 kDa isoform ( also called isoform # 4 ) of palladin is currently the best characterized and only variant of palladin that is ubiquitously expressed in epithelial cells 201 . In cultured cells , palladin localizes to various actin - rich structures such as contractile - based stress fibres and muscle sarcomeres as well as cell anchoring sites such as podosomes and focal adhesions 202 , 204 ( p8 ) . Palladin is also enriched at the leading edge of lamellipodia 205 . Structurally , palladin is composed of 3 - 5 ( isoform dependent ) immunoglobulin - like ( Ig ) domains . The actin - binding region of palladin has been linked specifically to Ig - like domains 3 and 4 of palladin and both of these domains display actin - crosslinking and bundling activities in vitro ( Figure 1 . 5 ) 186 , 187 . Specifically , three key lysine residues ( K15 , K18 and K51 ) within Ig - like domain 3 , control palladins actin - binding activity 187 . Furthermore , in cells expressing a palladin variant mutated at these three residues , actin cytoskeletal networks are significantly perturbed and cells display abnormal shape morphologies 187 . Similarly to α - actinin , palladin dimerizes upon actin - binding , and this likely facilitates its actin - crosslinking activity 188 . Currently , the remaining Ig - like domains of palladin show no role in crosslinking actin . Remarkably , recent biochemical studies out of the Beck lab has demonstrated that purified palladin , when co - incubated with actin monomers , generate branched F - actin arrays 206 . However , whether or not palladin can function as a bona fide actin nucleator ( like the Arp2 / 3 complex ) in living cells remains unknown . In addition to crosslinking and nucleating actin , palladin also functions as a cytosolic scaffolding protein by binding to actin and various actin - associated proteins such as α - actinin 207 , ezrin 202 , VASP 205 and CLP36 208 . As a scaffold , palladin likely recruits these actin - associated proteins to their normal destination within the cytoskeleton . Evidence for this comes from Gateva and colleagues , who showed that VASP - mediated actin stress fiber formation in U2OS cells depends on the presence of the VASP - binding domain of palladin 209 . 12 VASP VASP is another multi - functional actin - associated protein . VASP is found in various cell types and has important roles in various cell biological processes such as cell motility , filopodia formation and neuronal outgrowth 157 , 158 , 210 – 218 . Bacterial pathogens such as L . monocytogenes utilize VASP for their own efficient intracellular actin - based motility 219 – 221 . Functionally , VASP likely elongates actin filaments via a process termed “anti - capping” 216 , 222 . To do this , VASP binds the barbed ends of filaments and simultaneously blocks incoming capping proteins from terminating filament growth 216 , 223 . Tetramerization also imparts VASP with actin - bundling activity ; however , this activity is restricted in cells to regions such as the tips of filopodia 156 , 224 , 225 . Fascin Fascin is widely regarded as the prototypical actin - bundling protein found in protrusive cellular projections , namely filopodia , microvilli and cancer - specific invadopodia 226 – 232 . Unlike α - actinin and palladin , a single molecule of fascin possesses two separate actin - binding sites 189 , 190 . Consequently , actin - crosslinking by fascin generates more tightly packed bundles of F - actin 147 , 233 , 234 . In cells depleted of fascin , fewer filopodia and invadopodia are formed and these structures appear morphologically abnormal 147 , 232 . Functionally , the fascin - mediated tight bundling of parallel F - actin arrays likely imparts a high degree of stiffness to the protrusive actin structures . This is further supported by the finding of thicker and longer microvilli in cells overexpressing fascin 231 . 1 . 5 . 2 . Actin depolymerization factors and capping proteins While there are several mechanisms that promote actin nucleation and filament formation , cells do not possess an unlimited supply of actin monomers . Thus , the cytoskeleton relies on actin - binding proteins that sever filament ends , leading to the release of free actin molecules back into the cytosol . Unregulated and widespread growth of actin filaments in fact perturbs the proper function of actin - based structures . Therefore , the cell also utilizes a group of proteins which cap the ends of filaments ( commonly referred to as capping proteins ) . These capping proteins restrict the elongation of actin filaments to only productive regions such as the leading of the lamellipodia 4 . 13 ADF / cofilin Cofilin ( originally known as actin depolymerizing factor [ ADF ] ) , is one of the major actin severing proteins 235 . To do this , cofilin binds preferentially to the sides of filaments enriched with ADP - bound actin 23 . As a result , cofilin mainly severs actin from the pointed end of filaments 23 , 235 , 236 . As mentioned above , cofilin - mediated severing restores actin monomers back into the cytosol . In conjunction with the ability of profilin to accelerate the exchange of ADP for ATP , cofilin accelerates overall actin filament turnover rates 236 . Proper filament turnover plays a crucial part in motile structures ( described below ) such as lamellipodia , filopodia and some bacterially - derived structures 4 , 23 , 237 , 238 . 1 . 5 . 3 . Membrane - cytoskeletal linkers The physical connection of the actin cytoskeleton with the surface of the cell plays a crucial role in orchestrating many cell biological processes . Below , I will outline the major group of proteins that bind ( and link ) F - actin to components of the plasma membrane . ERM proteins – ezrin , radixin and moesin A small family of proteins , termed the ERM proteins , possess the ability to simultaneously bind components of the plasma membrane and filamentous actin . Importantly , this interaction directly links the actin cytoskeleton to the plasma membrane 239 , 240 . This group of proteins consists of ezrin , radixin and moesin 239 , 241 , 242 . Ezrin is the most studied of these proteins and will be examined in further detail in subsequent chapters of this thesis . In the cell , these proteins are commonly found at protrusive structures such as lamellipodia , filopodia and microvilli , as well as at focal adhesions . Ezrin is crucial for the ability of normal and cancer cells to migrate 239 , 240 , 243 – 248 . The bacterial pathogen , L . monocytogenes also relies on ezrin for the proper formation of their own motile actin - based structures 249 . Structurally , these proteins contain an N - terminal membrane binding site called the FERM domain which is followed by a small central linker region , and then , C - terminal F - actin binding site . Functionally , ERM proteins are locked in a closed ( non - actin and non - membrane binding ) inactive state 250 – 252 . Activation follows their direct interaction with phosphatidylinositol 4 , 5 - bisphosphate ( PIP2 ) lipid species at the plasma membrane 239 , 253 – 14 255 . F - actin binding by ERM proteins is further augmented by phosphorylation of the proteins at key threonine residues 256 , 257 . Spectrin Spectrin belongs to another group of actin - binding proteins that generate cytoskeletal - membrane networks . In fact , these networks are not solely limited to the plasma membrane as spectrin arrays also support other membrane - bound organelles such as the Golgi and nuclear envelope 258 – 267 . Spectrin networks are composed of α - and β - spectrin tetramers which bind to F - actin and the spectrin - associated proteins such as adducin , protein 4 . 1 and ankyrin 267 – 271 . 1 . 5 . 4 . Non - actin - binding proteins So far , we have covered components of the actin cytoskeleton that function as direct interactors of monomeric or filamentous actin . However , proper functioning of the actin cytoskeleton also relies on countless other factors that do not directly interact with it . These factors include plasma membrane receptors , molecular chaperones and various enzymes . Below are two non - actin - binding proteins that are generally not considered part of the actin cytoskeleton but are crucial in its proper functioning . We will examine these proteins in much more detail in chapters 4 through 7 . Cyclophilin A Cyclophilin A ( CypA ) is a small ( 18 kDa ) and abundantly expressed cytosolic ( and secreted ) eukaryotic enzyme 272 – 274 . CypA was the first characterized member of a family of 7 human molecular chaperones , termed the cyclophilins 275 – 277 . The initial studies of CypA came about from Handschumacher and colleagues who demonstrated that CypA was the binding partner of the immunosuppressive agent cyclosporine A ( CsA ) 276 . Today , the cyclophilins are well - known to possess peptidyl prolyl isomerase ( PPIase ) activity , which catalyzes the transformation of peptide bonds between trans and cis forms at proline residues 278 . Functionally , CypA is vital for several normal and pathological processes . Intracellularly , CypA is well known to participate in protein trafficking as well as protein folding ( and refolding ) processes 278 – 288 . CypA is a common target of several relevant human viral 15 pathogens such as HIV , hepatitis B , hepatitis C , influenza , vaccinia and cytomegalovirus 279 , 289 – 294 . During these infections , CypA interacts with various capsid and accessory components and these interactions ultimately lead to increased virion formation and infectivity of cells 279 , 285 , 295 , 296 . CypA is also secreted from cells and this form of CypA ( eCypA ) promotes the inflammatory response in cells 297 , 298 . CypA does not possess any intrinsic actin - binding activity ; however , several studies point towards CypA expression as an important factor for several cell biological processes that utilize actin , namely cell migration and cytoskeletal remodelling 274 , 299 , 300 . Despite these findings , the precise role of CypA during these actin - based events remains unclear . CD147 CD147 ( also called basigin or EMMPRIN ) is a plasma membrane receptor protein and well - known binding partner of CypA 301 . In humans , CD147 exists as 2 isoforms , basigin - 1 ( BGS - 1 ) and basigin - 2 ( BSG - 2 ) . BSG - 1 is retina - specific , whereas BSG - 2 is more widely expressed , thus the use of “CD147” commonly designates the latter variant 302 – 307 . Structurally , CD147 belongs to the immunoglobulin ( Ig ) superfamily of proteins as it consists of 2 extracellular facing Ig domains 302 . These extracellular Ig domains allow CD147 molecules to interact in a trans manner with components on the surface of other neighboring cells such as selectins and other CD147 molecules 302 , 306 , 308 , 309 . CD147 can also interact in a cis - like fashion with other membrane proteins such as monocarboxylate transporters 310 , 311 , CD44 312 , 313 , gamma - secretase 314 , NOD2 315 , and Syndecan - 1 316 . In the context of the actin cytoskeleton , CD147 expression in cell culture is required for the proper formation of several protrusive actin - rich structures such as lamellipodia , filopodia and invadopodia 317 – 320 . CD147 also mediates leukocyte migration ( chemotaxis ) via its interaction with extracellular CypA 321 – 323 . However , like CypA , CD147 does not possess any direct actin - binding capabilities , thus how might CD147 regulate motile actin - based dynamics ? Insight into this comes from Grass and colleagues investigation of invadopodia , whereby CD147 expression was found to maintain lipid rafts containing CD44 and epidermal growth factor receptor ( EGFR ) 312 . Activated EGFR within these rafts then triggers Arp2 / 3 - mediated actin polymerization through recruitment of the NPF cortactin 324 . Recent investigation into the actin - hijacking bacterium , Neisseria meningitidis ( N . meningitidis ) , has also uncovered direct binding between the cytoplasmic facing tail of 16 CD147 and the α - actinin family member , α - actinin 4 , 325 . This interaction facilitates actin filament assembly by the bacteria during their colonization of endothelial cells . Importantly , whether or not CD147 - α - actinin 4 complexes organizes actin filaments normally in cells remains unknown . 1 . 6 . Endocytosis The eukaryotic cell depends on the plasma membrane for much of its cellular signalling , and crucially , the uptake of membrane lipids , proteins and extracellular molecules 326 – 333 . Endocytosis involves a number of mechanisms by which the cell can internalize a wide variety of differently sized molecules , ranging from small growth factors and nutrients to whole cells 326 – 333 . Certain cell types , such as macrophages , can even engulf entire cells via a process termed efferocytosis 334 . Endocytosis also provides a means for the cell to recycle plasmalemmal components , such as transmembrane receptor proteins , back into the cell 328 , 330 , 331 , 335 – 337 . Given the wide range in cargo type ( and size ) , the cell has evolved various endocytic pathways , each distinguished by the size of particles they can internalize into the cell ( Figure 1 . 6 ) . Importantly , for many human pathogens , endocytosis represents the sole passageway into the host intracellular environment 338 – 346 . For the uptake of large cargo , such as extracellular bacteria or solutes / nutrients , the cell commonly utilizes two major mechanisms , phagocytosis and macropinocytosis respectively 330 . These pathways generally rely on actin cytoskeletal rearrangements at the membrane resulting in the formation of membranous protrusions or ruffles 6 , 330 . However , the cell can also internalize extracellular ligands such as signalling molecules and growth factors by forming small bulbous invaginations at the plasma membrane . This small - scale endocytic pathway utilizes small ( ~ 60 - 150 nm in diameter ) membrane vesicles to transport cargo into the cell 326 , 328 . Interestingly , several bacterial pathogens have evolved mechanisms to usurp the nanometer - scale size limit of these endocytic mechanisms during their infections 338 , 339 , 347 – 349 . Below , I will focus on the two major nanometer - scale endocytic mechanisms that cells rely on to internalize small cargo : 1 ) clathrin - mediated endocytosis , the most well - studied endocytic mechanism ( Figure 1 . 7 ) , and 2 ) caveolin - mediated endocytosis , a seemingly less vital endocytic pathway with emerging links to overall plasma membrane composition and dynamics ( Figure 1 . 8 ) . 17 1 . 6 . 1 . Clathrin - mediated endocytosis Clathrin - mediated endocytosis describes a receptor - mediated mechanism of endocytic vesicle formation . To accomplish this , a complex machinery of endocytic proteins assembles at the plasma membrane and carries out a finely - tuned procedure which involves curving of the membrane into an initial endocytic pit , formation of a structural coat around the growing invagination , elongation of the invagination , and eventually , freeing ( scission ) of the budding vesicle into the cytosol ( Figure 1 . 7 ) 328 , 329 , 333 . This endocytic process is characterized by the appearance of an ordered lattice of clathrin proteins around the forming vesicle 328 , 329 , 333 . Additionally , more than 50 different proteins from the cytosol are recruited over the course of the entire process 328 , 329 , 333 , 350 . Along with clathrin , these endocytic components are arranged into highly ordered and specific spatial arrangements about the membrane 328 , 329 , 333 , 350 . Additionally , many of these proteins ( including clathrin ) have been shown to arrive and leave in a specific timed sequence during vesicle formation 350 . Consequently , clathrin - mediated endocytosis can be broken - down into a few well - defined steps which will be outlined below ( Figure 1 . 7 ) . Following receptor - ligand interactions at the membrane , a cascade of events is initiated , beginning with the initiation of the formation of an endocytic invagination ( or bud ) . Clustering of a subset of endocytic - associated proteins at the site of vesicle formation provides a nucleation site for the assembly of the clathrin coat . These early coat proteins generally consist of scaffolding and adaptor proteins such as FCHo1 , FCHo2 , the AP2 complex , AP180 , Eps15 and intersectins 1 and 2 and epsin - 1 328 , 329 , 351 – 357 . Recruitment of these proteins generally depends on their binding to phospholipids , namely PIP2 , at the vesicle formation site 328 , 329 , 354 , 358 – 362 . The main function of these proteins is to 1 ) sculpt an initial pit by bending the plasma membrane 2 ) load cargo towards the site of vesicle formation and 3 ) recruit clathrin for coat formation 328 , 329 . Structurally , the BAR ( Bin / Amphiphysin / Rvs ) domain - containing proteins such as FCHO1 / 2 and amphiphysin , are intrinsically “banana - shaped” , and thus , upon their binding to the membrane , form it into a concave - like contour 352 , 363 . Epsin - 1 and AP180 interact with PIP2 via their helical epsin N - terminal homology ( ENTH ) domains 354 , 364 . Membrane bending is induced following the direct insertion of the ENTH domain into the lipid bilayer 364 . Eps15 also induces membrane bending through its Eps15 homology ( EH ) domain 365 , 366 . Important membrane cargos are recruited to the eventual vesicle - forming region through adaptors 18 such as AP2 , FCHo1 and Eps15 328 , 329 , 355 , 361 . Important cargo to be internalized are often modified via ubiquitylation and / or phosphorylation 367 – 369 . These modifications can then be recognized by proteins such as epsin - 1 369 . The major clathrin adaptor , AP2 , initiates clathrin recruitment and thus directly links the clathrin coat to the membrane - bound cargo 361 , 370 , 371 . Coat formation is further stabilized via AP2 interactions with Eps15 , as in cells expressing a non - AP2 binding mutant of Eps15 , endocytosis is inhibited 372 . The clathrin coat itself was first visualized by electron microscopy in 1964 by Roth and Porter , who discovered “bristle - coated” vesicles in mosquito oocytes 373 . It wasn’t until mid - 1970 when Barbara Pearse first isolated and characterized the bristle coat vesicle protein as clathrin 374 , 375 . Structurally , clathrin consists of a heavy and light chain 374 – 378 . Joining of three clathrin molecules into a triskelion shape constitutes the major subunit of the coat 378 , 379 . Interaction between multiple triskelia eventually form the characteristic polygonal lattice that is the clathrin coat 378 – 380 . As mentioned above , clathrin adapters such as AP2 , link the clathrin coat to the vesicle 361 , 370 , 371 , 381 . The clathrin coat could aid in membrane curvature induction ; however , given that clathrin does not directly insert into the membrane , these forces are believed to be insignificant 382 . The final two steps in the endocytic process involve scission of the budding vesicle from the plasma membrane , and subsequently , disassembly of the clathrin coat from the free vesicle . Separation of the budding vesicle from the plasma membrane classically involves the GTPase dynamin 383 – 390 . To do this , dynamin molecules assemble into a tight helical - shaped collar around the neck - region which links the circular vesicle to the plasma membrane 384 – 386 . Using GTP , dynamin pinches the neck - region until the vesicle is finally resolved 387 – 390 . Following this , the molecular chaperone , heat shock cognate 70 ( Hsc70 ) , and its binding partner , auxilin , interact with and dislodge the clathrin coat , finally releasing the vesicle into the cytosol 350 , 391 – 394 . 1 . 6 . 2 . Caveolin - mediated endocytosis There exist several mechanisms of small - scale endocytosis that do not rely on clathrin 326 , 327 , 330 – 332 , 395 , 396 . These pathways , commonly referred to as clathrin - independent endocytosis ( CIE ) , utilize a different array of proteins as well as function beyond simply internalizing cargo 326 , 327 , 330 – 332 , 335 ( p6 ) , 395 , 397 , 398 , 399 ( p2 ) , 400 ( p2 ) , 401 . Of these CIE mechanisms , the most well - studied is caveolin - mediated endocytosis ( Figure 1 . 8 ) . However , as well will see 19 below , there exists some controversy in the field as to the exact extent by which cells employ caveolin endocytosis to internalize cargo 326 , 327 , 402 – 404 . The origins of caveolin - mediated endocytosis can be traced back to the fine examination of endothelial tissue by George Palade in 1953 405 . Palade’s electron micrographs depicted small ( ~ 50 - 100 nm diameter ) flask - shaped invaginations of the plasma membrane 405 . In 1955 , Yamada identified similar “small pit - like depressions” ( or invaginations ) in gall bladder epithelia , coining the name caveolae or “little caves” 406 . Since then , caveolae have been observed in many cell types and are especially abundant in endothelial , muscle and fat cells 327 , 405 . Early ultrastructural examination of caveolae often revealed a filament - or striated - like coat surrounding the cytoplasmic surface of the structures 407 – 410 . It wasn’t until 1992 when Rothberg and colleagues ( out of Richard Anderson’s lab ) identified the first caveolae coat protein , which they named caveolin 411 . In their study , immunofluorescence localization of caveolin was depicted as small puncta in the cell 411 . Indeed , immunogold electron microscopy confirmed caveolin presence at caveolae 411 . There are three caveolin isoforms in humans , caveolins 1 - 3 . Caveolin - 1 ( Cav1 ) , the archetypal variant , is expressed in various non - muscle cells , and importantly , is crucial for caveolar biogenesis . The evidence for this comes from Cav1 overexpression studies showing 1 ) the restoration of caveolae to Cav1 knockout cells and 2 ) the de novo formation of caveolae in cells that do not normally generate them 327 . However , overexpression of Cav1 in endothelial cells does not increase their number of caveolae 327 . Furthermore , there are suggestions that caveolin expression is not entirely prognosticate of caveolae as certain cells types express caveolin but do not possess any noticeable caveolae 412 – 415 . Caveolin - 2 ( Cav2 ) is expressed similarly to Cav1 but is dispensable for caveolar formation ; as of yet , its exact cellular function remains unclear 326 , 416 . Caveolin - 3 ( Cav3 ) is a muscle specific variant , and like Cav1 , is a crucial factor in caveolae formation as mice knocked - out for Cav3 show a loss of sarcolemma caveolae 417 – 419 . Similar to the formation of clathrin - coated pits , caveolae are comprised of , and depend on , accessory proteins ( Figure 1 . 8 ) 326 , 327 , 399 ( p2 ) . The major accessory to caveolin are the cavin family of proteins , of which there are four members , cavins 1 - 4 327 , 401 , 420 – 424 . Although cavin - 4 is muscle specific , all cavins have been identified at caveolae 327 , 401 , 421 – 424 . Functionally , the cavins are required for caveolae generation 400 , 401 , 425 – 428 . Additionally , 20 Cav1 expression and proper localization to the plasma membrane appears to depend on cavin expression in some cases 327 , 400 . An important feature of cavins is their ability to form complexes 401 , 426 , 429 . However , the exact degree to which caveolae are coated by cavin complexes remains muddied by several findings . Firstly , not all Cav1 plasma membrane puncta contain the cavins 426 . Secondly , Cav1 has also been shown to be absent at various cavin puncta 401 , 426 . Lastly , cavins can complex at the plasma membrane even in the absence of caveolin 430 . Thus , the exact role of cavins alongside caveolin remains a complicated puzzle so far unsolved . Four additional proteins are well - founded as bona fide caveolar components : actin , dynamin , EHD2 and pacsin 2 ( Figure 1 . 8 ) 341 , 397 , 399 ( p2 ) , 429 , 431 , 432 . However , the precise interplay between these proteins as well as their role during caveolae coat formation ( and internalization ) remains unclear as of yet . Fluorescent - phalloidin staining of cells has revealed F - actin puncta at caveolar - like structures 433 . Furthermore , proper caveolae dynamics , such as their intracellular transport of cargo , appears to depend on actin , and more precisely , the actin crosslinker filamin A 434 – 436 . Curiously , stable arrest of caveolae at the plasma membrane also depends on filamin A 434 , 437 . The exact role of actin at caveolae is further complicated by the fact that EHD2 , a dynamin - like ATPase , is a negative regulator of caveolae internalization 399 , 438 . The exact mechanism behind the EHD2 - mediated restriction is unknown . In 2013 , Ludwig and colleagues set out to define the composition and ultrastructure of the caveolar coat 429 . Like dynamin , EHD2 localizes to the neck - like stalk of caveolae , but itself is excluded and dispensable for caveolin - cavin coat complex formation 429 . Pacsin 2 is another key regulator of caveolae formation as in cells depleted of pacsin 2 , caveolae are depleted and caveolin proteins accumulate in the membrane 397 . However , like EHD2 , pacsin 2 is excluded from the caveolar coat complex 429 ; consequently , the exact mechanism governing EHD2 - and pacsin 2 - mediated caveolae formation remains vague . As we have seen , caveolae were first characterized as plasma membrane vesicles 405 . In light of this , to what degree are caveolae genuinely utilized by the cell to internalize cargo ? Early GFP - Cav1 overexpression studies suggested that caveolae were , in fact , static structures when compared to the highly motile clathrin coated pits 439 , 440 . Contrary to this , Pelkmans and Zerial later reported on two dynamically distinct pools of caveolae , 1 ) a more immobile pool as described in earlier reports , and importantly , 2 ) a highly dynamic pool which maintained their caveolar coat during continuous cycles of fusion and fission 21 at the plasma membrane 441 . Using genome editing techniques , Shvets and colleagues revealed that only a small percentage ( ~ 5 % ) of caveolae undergo endocytosis 442 . To make matters more difficult , very few bona fide caveolar ligands exist in the literature 443 – 445 , 446 ( p ) , 447 – 449 . Be that as it may , strong evidence for caveolin - mediated intracellular cargo transport does exist in transcytosis of endothelial cells 211 , 326 , 408 , 450 – 455 . As we will see in chapter 1 . 6 . 4 , several pathogens also utilize caveolin - mediated endocytosis for their entry into cells 339 , 340 , 342 – 344 , 456 . If the cell sparsely utilizes caveolae to internalize cargo , what are its other functions ? Several studies support a role for caveolae as signalling platforms 326 , 457 – 467 . Of particular interest is the idea that caveolae can function as a guard against cell rupture due to plasma membrane stretching . In their examination of frog sarcomeres , Dulhunty and Franzini - Armstrong proposed that caveolae could serve as a reservoir of membrane , and that upon tension events , such as sarcomere stretching , collapse back into the plasma membrane 468 . In 2011 , Sinha and colleagues demonstrated flattening of caveolae back into the plasma membrane in response to mechanical stress experienced by the cell 403 . In a series of simple yet elegant experiments , Sinha et al . incubated cells in a hypo - osmotic medium leading to their swelling . Using a combination of Total Internal Reflection Fluorescence ( TIRF ) and electron microscopy they demonstrated a significant 50 % reduction in the number of caveolae following exposure of cells to the hypo - osmotic medium 403 . Importantly , this mechanoprotective role for caveolae has also been demonstrated in vivo 469 , 470 . 1 . 6 . 3 . F - actin during endocytosis The Arp2 / 3 - mediated actin polymerization machinery is among the vast array of endocytic components which controls the invagination process during clathrin - and caveolin - mediated endocytosis 95 , 96 . However , initial invagination formation by the endocytic machinery does not appear to depend on actin filament polymerization 6 . Rather , the Arp2 / 3 complex comes into play at later stages when the invagination needs to be pushed away from the plasma membrane prior to vesicle scission ( Figure 1 . 9 ) 6 , 99 . One set of models for this places actin filament barbed ends that are pointed towards the plasma membrane . As the growing filament pushes against the membrane , the other end , attached to the budding vesicle , pushes the invagination deeper into the cell 6 , 329 ( Figure 1 . 9 ) . Another model involves the use of type I myosins pulling on the actin filaments to 22 drive the invagination inwards ( Figure 1 . 9 ) 6 , 8 . The experimental support for the role of F - actin during endocytosis comes from the observation of fewer endocytic cups ( or pits ) in cells treated with filamentous actin inhibitors such as latrunculin A 9 , 471 – 473 . In these studies , the few invaginations that were observed following drug treatment appear shallower and seemingly stalled at the plasma membrane . 1 . 7 . Listeria monocytogenes A great deal of our current understanding of eukaryotic cell biology ( and molecular biology ) takes its source from the study of bacteria . One of the most prominent of these bacteria is the human pathogen L . monocytogenes 106 , 474 – 479 . In this section , I will review L . monocytogenes , its prevalence amongst humans , an overview of its infectious cycle and the ways by which researchers study L . monocytogenes infections . In chapter 1 . 8 , I will provide extensive insight into the molecular mechanisms that are currently known which control the L . monocytogenes infectious cycle . 1 . 7 . 1 . L . monocytogenes – A relevant human pathogen In 1924 , bacteriologist E . G . D . Murray and colleague’s first isolated L . monocytogenes from infected rabbits and guinea pigs 480 . In this study , Murray et al . characterized L . monocytogenes , then a new species ( given the name Bacterium monocytogenes ) , as Gram - positive and rod - shaped . Following this , L . monocytogenes was recognised to cause meningitis and abortion in ruminants 481 – 485 . It wasn’t until much later that L . monocytogenes was accepted as a food - borne disease - causing agent in both animals and humans 481 , 484 , 486 – 488 . Compared to other well - known human pathogens , the number of L . monocytogenes infections worldwide is low ( ~ 23 , 000 in 2010 ) 488 , 489 . However , based off of reports from within North America , comparisons to other food - borne pathogens show that the mortality rate of L . monocytogenes infected individuals is high ( ~ 20 - 30 % ) ; thus , even to this day , L . monocytogenes remains a genuine cause for concern in humans 488 . During high titre ( 10 9 bacteria ) human infections , immunocompetent adults generally show symptoms of gastroenteritis 490 . In contrast to this , low dose infections ( ~ 10 2 - 10 4 ) of children , elderly , immunocompromised and pregnant women show much greater symptom severity as they are at risk for sepsis , meningitis , fetal infection and miscarriage 488 . L . monocytogenes is also a matter of grave concern for the food industry 23 as it can survive ( and propagate ) in conditions of low temperature ( and pH ) as well as high salt concentrations 487 , 488 , 491 . Following ingestion of contaminated food products , L . monocytogenes bacteria primarily target and invade into the host intestinal epithelia . Once inside cells , L . monocytogenes become motile and spread laterally within the epithelia or below into the lamina propria . In the lamina propria , L . monocytogenes can enter the lymph , and then bloodstream , where it can eventually target other organs such as the liver and spleen 475 . L . monocytogenes can also target the central nervous system by crossing the blood - brain barrier 492 . Resolution of infections can be achieved by administration of β - lactam or aminoglycoside antibiotics 486 . 1 . 7 . 2 . The intracellular lifecycle of L . monocytogenes Figure 1 . 10 depicts the intracellular lifecycle of L . monocytogenes during infection of mammalian cells . Over the past 30 years , microscopic examination of cell culture infections has been the key tool in elucidating each of the various stages of L . monocytogenes infection 493 . Validation using in vivo infection models and various molecular biology techniques has also proven to be indispensable 52 , 105 , 107 , 494 , 495 . L . monocytogenes can invade both phagocytic and non - phagocytic cells 478 , 493 , 496 – 505 . Phagocytes such macrophages utilize phagocytosis to internalize the bacteria 506 – 508 . To gain access to the intracellular environment of non - phagocytic cells , such as the intestinal epithelia , L . monocytogenes co - opts both the host receptor - mediated endocytic machinery and actin cytoskeleton 338 , 347 , 348 , 478 , 493 . Specifically , L . monocytogenes express on their cell surface two major effector proteins , Internalin A ( InlA ) and B ( InlB ) 478 , 497 , 509 . Interaction of InlA and / or InlB with their corresponding host cell receptor proteins triggers clathrin recruitment and actin cytoskeletal rearrangements at the bacteria - cell contact site ( Figure 1 . 11 ) 338 , 347 , 348 , 510 . Rapidly following this , actin polymerization pushes the plasma membrane up and over the attached bacteria in a process termed “zippering” internalization 338 , 510 . Once internalized , and only for a short period of time , the L . monocytogenes bacteria remain within a vacuole 493 . In order to rupture the vacuole and escape into the host cell cytosol , L . monocytogenes release several pore - forming effectors 511 – 514 . In other cell types , L . monocytogenes have been demonstrated to remain inside of vacuoles to propagate infection 515 . For example , in macrophages L . monocytogenes can remain inside and replicate within compartments termed spacious Listeria - containing phagosomes ( SLAPs ) 516 . Inside intestinal goblet cells , L . monocytogenes has been 24 shown to remain inside vacuoles which then transcytose the bacteria directly into the lamina propria 517 . The next step for bacteria that have gained access to the host cytosol is their replication and then actin - based motility 493 . In order to become motile , L . monocytogenes preferentially express at one of their poles the surface protein ActA 518 , 519 . Using ActA , the bacteria recruit and activate the Arp2 / 3 - based actin polymerization machinery 52 , 105 , 520 . Polymerization of actin filaments at one pole of the bacteria generates a slender structure commonly referred to as a comet ( or rocket ) tail ( Figure 1 . 12 ) 474 , 479 , 493 , 520 , 521 . These actin - rich comet tails propel the bacteria within the host cytosol . Importantly , when these structures reach the plasma membrane , they deform it around the comet tail into a finger - like structure called a membrane protrusion 115 , 476 , 493 . Projection of the bacterial - containing membrane protrusion into adjacent host cells allows for the direct cell - to - cell transfer of the bacteria . Once inside the newly infected host cell , the bacteria reside within a double - membraned vacuole which is also broken down by bacterial pore forming effectors , thus freeing the bacteria into cytosol so as to propagate further infection 477 , 493 . 1 . 7 . 3 . Models of L . monocytogenes infection In vivo models Infections of mice represent the most commonly used in vivo small animal model of listeriosis . Intravenous ( i . v . ) inoculation of the mice is widely utilized by researchers given its high level of reproducibility ( along with low variability ) of infection 522 . Importantly , compared to the oral route , the LD 50 for i . v . inoculation is low ( ~ 10 4 - 10 6 bacteria ) 522 , 523 . The precise route of inoculation is direct injection into the lateral tail vein . During these infections , bacteria reach can reach the spleen or liver within 10 - 15 minutes 524 – 526 . It isn’t until 24 - 48 hours post i . v . inoculation when bacteria are detected in the gut . Interestingly , mouse infected via the i . v . route generally do not show bacterial dissemination to the brain 527 . Oral transmission of L . monocytogenes usually occurs via the use of contaminated food . One major pitfall of the oral route is that it requires an infectious dose that is generally multiple fold greater than that used during i . v . inoculations . One reason for this lies in the discovery of a single point mutation in the murine receptor of InlA , E - cadherin , which caused limited infection of the mice 528 . To combat this , researchers have developed both 25 mouse - adapted ( murinized ) bacteria 529 and transgenic humanized mice 530 , 531 . Importantly , these transgenic tools have demonstrated increased infection in the mouse intestine 530 , 531 . Interestingly , a recent developed infection model using wild - type mice and bacteria deleted for the InlA gene has been demonstrated to establish gut colonization without the risk of rapid systemic infection that is commonly caused using conventional oral infection models 532 . This new model also causes brain infection within a week 532 . Gerbils represent another relevant animal model to study infection by L . monocytogenes 530 , 533 . In fact , gerbils are a natural host of the bacteria , and are fatally susceptible to listeriosis 530 . Disson and colleagues’ investigation of gerbils revealed that , unlike mice , gerbils do not have a species barrier for infection via the InlA - or InlB - mediated invasion process 530 . Specifically , gerbil intestinal epithelial cells co - express E - cadherin and Met 530 . Consequently , both isogenic L . monocytogenes mutants (  inlA and  inlB ) , can infect cells to a high degree 530 . Importantly , oral inoculation of gerbils results in successful bacterial crossing of the intestinal barrier 530 . Gerbils are also permissive to fetoplacental infection following oral inoculation with wild - type L . monocytogenes bacteria 530 . Although gerbils appear an easier model to study infections by L . monocytogenes , the lack of gerbil specific reagents , such as primary antibodies , limits their potential usefulness to researchers . In vitro models In spite of their physiological relevance , animal model infections are expensive , laborious and require extensive training ; thus , the exploitation of cells in culture represent a quick and easy method to observe ( and study ) infection . Many immortalized cell lines are susceptible to infection by L . monocytogenes , and in conjunction with various microscopic imaging techniques , all of the intracellular stages of infection can also be visualized 249 , 338 , 347 , 493 , 497 – 500 , 534 . The combination of gene silencing ( or chemical inhibitors ) with various microbiological techniques , such as gentamicin protection assays , plaque assays and foci assays , provides an extremely useful way for researchers to identify crucial host and bacterial components of infection 114 , 249 , 494 , 515 , 534 – 540 . Thus , following the in vitro identification of a crucial host ( or bacterial ) factor of infection , researchers can then shift towards the use of animal models to ascertain that factors physiological relevance . 26 1 . 8 . Molecular mechanisms controlling L . monocytogenes receptor - mediated endocytosis and actin - based motility As mentioned earlier , L . monocytogenes express on their cell surface ( as well as secrete ) a variety of effector proteins that influence their ability to initially infect host cells , and once inside them , to gain motility to spread amongst them . Following ~ 30 years of dedicated study to these effectors , we now know many of their specific host cell targets . In this section , I will review the molecular mechanisms underlying these host - pathogen interactions as well as the various actin - based structures that L . monocytogenes generate as a result of them . 1 . 8 . 1 . Cellular invasion L . monocytogenes actively invades various non - phagocytic cells both in vivo and in cell culture 478 . To accomplish this , L . monocytogenes express on their cell surface two members of the internalin family of proteins , InlA and InlB 478 , 497 , 541 – 543 . Although the initial signalling cascades during bacterial internalization differ for both effector proteins , both ultimately end in the activation of clathrin - and Arp2 / 3 - mediated machineries at the bacteria - host contact site ( Figure 1 . 11 ) 347 , 478 , 544 . InlA - mediated invasion The host adhesion molecule , E - cadherin , is the cellular receptor of InlA 542 . E - cadherin is involved in adherens junction formation in various tissues such as the intestinal epithelial , placenta and blood - brain barrier 545 , 546 . In the host intestine , a process known as cell extrusion sheds old epithelial cells at the tips of villi 547 . Importantly , L . monocytogenes hijacks this process as E - cadherin molecules are exposed in the neighboring cells during cell shedding 548 . In addition to this internalization mechanism , L . monocytogenes bacteria can simply interact with E - cadherin molecules that are expressed on the apical surface of host epithelial cells 549 . Historically , L . monocytogenes bacteria often did not display invasion of mouse cell culture models 528 . Molecular examination of E - cadherin revealed a proline residue at position of 16 which was crucial for invasion of human , gerbil , or guinea pig cells but not mice or rats 528 . Further investigations showed the amino acid glutamic acid in place of proline at 27 position 16 of mouse ( and rat ) E - cadherin and this mutation precluded InlA recognition and subsequent invasion by L . monocytogenes 528 . The successful generation of a transgenic mice line expressing a humanized version of E - cadherin facilitated infection of the animal gut 531 . As mentioned earlier , researchers have also generated a L . monocytogenes strain which expresses a murinized form InlA ( Inl m ) , thus providing another means by which to study in vivo mouse gut infections . InlA - E - cadherin interactions result in receptor clustering at the bacteria - host contact site , and importantly , activation of E - cadherin by two post - translational modifications 550 . Specifically , the host kinase Src catalyzes the tyrosine phosphorylation of E - cadherin whereas ubiquitination occurs via the host ubiquitin - ligase Hakai 550 . Importantly , E - cadherin activation is required for the subsequent recruitment of the clathrin endocytic machinery as well as dynamic actin cytoskeletal remodelling ( see below ) during bacterial invasion 550 . InlB - mediated invasion InlB facilitates L . monocytogenes invasion into various cell culture lines , and importantly , in the absence ( or proper expression ) of E - cadherin by the host 478 , 497 – 500 , 541 . Thus , the use of cell lines such as HeLa , CHO , HepG2 and Vero allows researchers to examine the underlying molecular mechanisms behind the InlB - mediated invasion process without interference by InlA 478 . The major cellular receptor of InlB is the hepatocyte growth factor receptor ( HGFR ) Met 543 . Functionally , therefore , InlB mimics HGF , the natural ligand of Met 551 – 553 . The InlB - mediated invasion process also shows species tropism as InlB binding to Met occurs only within humans , mice and gerbils 554 . The rabbit and guinea pig Met receptors do not respond to InlB 554 . Like the InlA - mediated process , post translational modification of Met following bacterial adhesion triggers clathrin and actin recruitment to the bacteria - host contact site 338 , 347 , 348 . Rather than hijacking the host kinase Src , Met is autophosphorylated 347 , 478 . Met autophosphorylation then triggers recruitment of the host ubiquitin ligase , Cbl , which in turn ubiquitinates Met 347 . Clathrin and actin during bacterial invasion 28 The host clathrin endocytic machinery is crucial for L . monocytogenes entry into cells 347 . Clathrin itself , and its accessory proteins such as dynamin and Dab2 are recruited to bacteria - host contact sites following receptor activation 347 . Using siRNA , Veiga and colleagues demonstrated a crucial role for clathrin endocytic components as in cells depleted of clathrin , Eps15 , dynamin , Grb2 , Hrs or CD2AP , bacterial invasion is significantly perturbed 347 . Dynamin - mediated recruitment of cortactin , a potent Arp2 / 3 complex activator , eventually triggers actin cytoskeletal rearrangements beneath the site of bacteria - host contact 347 . Additionally , the clathrin - associated protein Hip1R as well as myosin VI , serve as linkage between clathrin and the actin cytoskeleton 348 . Other actin - associated factors such as the Rho GTPases Rac1 and Cdc42 also augment Arp2 / 3 complex activation during bacterial internalization events 478 , 544 , 555 – 557 . These factors depend on the generation of phosphoinositide’s such as PIP2 and PIP3 at the bacterial contact site as in cells treated with PI 3 - kinase inhibitors , such as wortmannin or LY294002 , bacterial internalization levels are decreased 558 . Normal actin polymerization dynamics such as F - actin elongation and depolymerization are also important for L . monocytogenes entry into cells as shown by inhibition studies involving VASP and ADF / cofilin 556 , 559 . Actin polymerization against the plasma membrane eventually engulfs the attached bacteria in a “zipper - like” fashion 478 , 560 . Interestingly , components of the host exocytic machinery have recently been highlighted to promote bacterial entry into cells 561 . Furthermore , septin proteins , which can assemble into filaments , have been described to promote the L . monocytogenes internalization process 540 , 562 . Besides InlA and InlB , Ghosh and colleagues recently characterized InlF as a crucial factor for the intermediate filament - mediated entry of L . monocytogenes into the brain 563 . 1 . 8 . 2 . Vacuolar escape Following the internalin - mediated invasion process , L . monocytogenes bacteria reside within a vacuole 493 . This represents only a short period of the infectious process as L . monocytogenes lyse the vacuole within ~ 30 minutes 564 , 565 . To rupture and escape the vacuole , L . monocytogenes bacteria secrete the pore - forming toxin listeriolysin O ( LLO ) 511 . Infection studies using a mutant strain of L . monocytogenes that does not express LLO shows the bacteria trapped within vacuoles following their invasion into the host cell 512 . L . monocytogenes also secrete two additional vacuole breaking enzymes , phospholipase A and B , whose expression is essential following initial invasion as well as 29 cell - to - cell spreading events when the bacteria reside within double - membraned vacuoles 478 , 481 , 513 , 514 , 566 , 567 . Escape into the host cytosol allows L . monocytogenes bacteria to replicate prior to their actin - based motility . However , as mentioned above , L . monocytogenes also exploit these vacuoles to gain access to the basement membrane of the intestinal epithelia , or in macrophages , as an additional habitat to replicate within 475 , 517 . Recent findings by Kortebi and colleagues also demonstrates a persistent population of L . monocytogenes bacteria residing within vacuoles following multi - day cell culture infections 515 . In this study , intercellular spreading bacteria were observed to downregulate ActA expression , and instead , enter a slow replicative state within Listeria - Containing Vacuoles ( LisCVs ) 515 . 1 . 8 . 3 . Actin - based motility – Comet / Rocket tail formation Arguably the most characteristic feature of the L . monocytogenes infectious cycle is the ability of the bacteria to commandeer the host Arp2 / 3 - mediated actin polymerization machinery for their intracellular motility 52 , 105 , 107 , 520 .
